5 min
Which Wise Person? The Obligation Depends on Need
Al-Nasafi asks which type of wise person the Mu'tazilite argument targets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi distinguishes between a needy wise person and a self-sufficient one, showing that the Mu'tazilite obligation does not apply to God.

Core Idea
A self-sufficient wise person is not obligated like a dependent commander.

Al-Nasafi then poses a crucial question: to which kind of wise person does the Mu'tazilite obligation apply? He presents two alternatives—a needy wise person or a self-sufficient wise person—and shows that neither supports the Mu'tazilite conclusion. This lesson explores this second refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and alternatives
Al-Nasafi asks: upon which wise person does the obligation to provide all means fall? Read the two alternatives carefully.
The first alternative describes a dependent, weak commander; the second describes an independent, mighty one.
2
Understand the responses to each alternative
If the Mu'tazilites choose the first (needy wise person), al-Nasafi concedes the premise but says it cannot be extended to God. If they choose the second (self-sufficient), he denies the premise because a self-sufficient being is not obligated in that way.
Note the phrases 'فهو مسلم ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب' and 'فهو ممنوع'.
3
Draw the conclusion
The Mu'tazilites are forced into a dilemma: either their argument applies only to needy commanders (not God) or it is simply false for the self-sufficient. Either way, their objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة أخرى للمعتزلة والرد عليها فأما ما اعتمدوا عليه من الشبه فنقول قولهم إنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إلى آخر ما حكينا. كلام باطل من وجوه. أحدها: أنه استدل على هذا بالشاهد من غير إثبات التسوية فيقال له: هب أن الأمر في الشاهد كان على ما زعمت، ولكن لم ينبغي أن يكون الأمر كذلك في الغائب؟ وبأي معنى تجمع بينهما؟ فإن زعمت أن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد كان إثباته في الغائب واجبًا من غير اعتبار المعنى، فذلك باطل وقد بينا قبل هذا أن من رأى عبده يزني بأمته فلم يمنع مع القدرة على المنع بل اشتغل بتهيئة أسباب ذلك كان ديوثًا سفيها وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه، وكذا في الشاهد لا قدرة لفاعل ما على إيجاد الأجسام وإنما يقدر الفاعل في الشاهد على بعض الأعراض وعندكم لا يقدر أيضا إلا على إثبات الأفعال في محال هذه الأجسام فلو قسم الغائب على الشاهد لبطل القول بخلق الأجسام وهو قول الدهرية. وإن أثبتم ذلك في الغائب وإن امتنع إثبات مثله في الشاهد أبطلتم كلامكم. ثم يقال: على أي حكيم يجب ما ذكرت من بذل جميع ما في مقدوره لمن دعاه إلى طاعته وطلب رجوعه عن معصيته؟ أعلى حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة المطيع، ورجوع العدو عن العداوة متعذر بكثرة أعوانه وأنصاره وتكاتف حزبه وأوليائه ذليل عند كثرة أعدائه؟ أم على حكيم مستغن عن طاعة غيره إياه عزيز في ذاته قوي في سلطانه لا يضعفه كثرة الأعداء ولا يقويه كثرة الأولياء؟ فإن قالوا بالأول فهو مسلم ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب. وإن قالوا بالثاني فهو ممنوع ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم أن الأمر كان مريدًا لطاعة المأمور محبًا لها، وكذا لرجوع العدو لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ويبذل ما يحتاج إليه المأمور للامتثال ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل
Another objection of the Mu'tazila and the refutation of it: As for what they rely on of the objections, we say: their statement, 'We found that the wise person, when he commands obedience, loves it and desires it, it is not permissible for him to withhold from the commanded what enables him to perform his obedience,' up to what we related. This is false speech from several aspects. One of them: it deduces this from the seen without establishing equivalence. It is said to him: suppose that the matter in the seen was as you claim, but why should the matter be thus in the unseen? And by what meaning do you combine between them? If you claim that everything established in the seen must be established in the unseen without considering the meaning, that is false. And we have shown before this that whoever sees his slave committing adultery with his slave girl and does not prevent him despite ability to prevent, but rather busies himself with preparing the means for that, is a cuckold and foolish. And in the unseen the matter is contrary to that. Likewise, in the seen, no agent has power to create bodies, but rather the agent in the seen is only capable of some accidents, and according to you he is also only capable of creating actions in the loci of these bodies. So if the unseen is measured by the seen, the statement of creation of bodies would be invalid, which is the statement of the materialists. And if you establish that in the unseen, and the establishment of its like in the seen is impossible, you invalidate your speech. Then it is said: upon which wise person does what you mentioned become obligatory, i.e., expending all that is in his power to one who calls him to his obedience and seeks his return from his disobedience? Is it upon a wise person who is in need of the obedience of the obedient, and the return of the enemy from enmity is made difficult by the large number of his supporters and helpers and the cohesion of his party and allies, and he is humiliated by the large number of his enemies? Or upon a wise person who is independent of the obedience of others to him, mighty in His essence, strong in His authority, whom the abundance of enemies does not weaken nor the abundance of allies strengthen? If they say the first, it is conceded, but there is no way to extend it to the unseen. And if they say the second, it is denied. Then we say to them: if the matter were as you mentioned, that the commander desires the obedience of the commanded and loves it, and likewise the return of the enemy, then what you said would be conceded, and he would indeed give and expend what the commanded needs for compliance. But why do you say that the Creator (al-Barr)...
Source pages p. 1017

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite shubha (objection). The Mu'tazilites argue that a wise commander who commands obedience and desires it must provide the means for that obedience; otherwise, he would be acting foolishly. Al-Nasafi responds by pointing out multiple flaws. First, the argument relies on an unqualified analogy between the seen world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). He gives two examples: a man who sees his slave committing adultery and does nothing is considered a cuckold (dayyuth), but this does not apply to God; also, in the seen world no agent can create bodies, only accidents, whereas God creates bodies. So the analogy fails. Second, he asks which kind of wise person the obligation applies to: a needy wise person who depends on others, or a self-sufficient wise person? If the Mu'tazilites say the first, the analogy cannot be extended to God; if they say the second, their premise is denied because a self-sufficient wise person is not obligated in the same way. Third, even if we concede the premise, the Mu'tazilites still have not proven that God withholds means; in fact, God provides all necessary guidance, but His wisdom is not subject to human expectations.

Key Terms
حكيم
محتاج
مستغن
تعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi respond if the Mu'tazilites choose the second alternative (self-sufficient wise person)?
A) He concedes it but says it cannot be extended to the unseen.
B) He denies it outright.
C) He accepts it fully.
D) He ignores it.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite obligation only applies to needy commanders, not to the self-sufficient God, so their argument does not hold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself seeking people's approval as if you need their obedience to feel secure? How does the description of the self-sufficient God challenge you to rely solely on His pleasure rather than human praise?
?
The Mu'tazilites mistakenly thought God was like a needy commander. In what areas of your life do you unconsciously think you are doing God a favor by worshipping Him, or that He needs your worship? How can you shift your mindset to one of pure gratitude?
?
When you face trials or unanswered prayers, do you ever feel entitled to God's help, as if He owes you because of your obedience? How does Al-Nasafi's point about God's self-sufficiency and independence reshape your expectations and trust in His wisdom?

4 min
Even If Premise Granted, the Conclusion Does Not Follow
Al-Nasafi presents a conditional argument: even if the premise is true, the conclusion is not proven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if the Mu'tazilite premise were true, they still have not proven that God withholds means of obedience.

Core Idea
Conceding the premise does not concede the conclusion.

In his final refutation, al-Nasafi assumes the Mu'tazilite premise is correct for argument's sake, but then points out that they still have not shown that God actually withholds anything. This lesson examines that conditional argument and its implication: the burden of proof remains on the Mu'tazilites.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Al-Nasafi says: if the matter were as you claim (that the commander desires obedience and loves it), then indeed he would provide means. But the Mu'tazilites have not shown that God withholds means.
The key phrase is 'لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم ... لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ... ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل'.
2
Identify the missing link
The Mu'tazilites assume that because God commands obedience, He must provide means, but they do not prove that He fails to do so. Al-Nasafi turns their argument back on them.
The passage cuts off after 'لم قلتم أن البار جل', but the gist is clear: why do you claim that God does not provide means?
3
Understand the burden of proof
Even if the Mu'tazilite reasoning were sound, they still need to establish that God does not provide necessary guidance. Al-Nasafi challenges them to prove that omission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does al-Nasafi concede in this conditional argument?
A) That the Mu'tazilite premise is false.
B) That if the premise were true, then the commander would provide means.
C) That God withholds means.

Even if the Mu'tazilite reasoning is accepted, they still have not proven that God withholds means; the burden remains on them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted an argument from someone because it sounded logical, only to later realize that the conclusion wasn't actually proven? How did that affect your trust in reasoning? What will you do next time to avoid being misled by a conditional that doesn't hold?
?
When you find yourself disagreeing with someone about religious matters, do you check whether their premises lead inevitably to their conclusion, or do you sometimes accept the premise too quickly? How can you train yourself to pause and ask, 'Even if that were true, does it prove what they claim?'

Refuting the Claim of Equal Intellect
Nasafi refutes equal intellect using observed disparities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses observable disparities in intellect and a reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that all people have equal intellect.

Core Idea
Observing real intellectual differences demolishes the claim of equal intellect.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that God does not favor anyone with a greater intellect and that all people are created equal in mental capacity. Nasafi finds this absurd and points to what we see every day: some people are brilliant and learn quickly, while others are dull and slow. He then shows that accepting this claim leads to even more ridiculous conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Identify the exact doctrine Nasafi is refuting: that God does not grant any person more intellect than another, and that all intellects are equal in creation.
2
Observe Nasafi's counter-evidence
Notice that he appeals to observable reality: some people have abundant intellect, sharp minds, and strong understandings, while others are dull and slow-witted.
He gives specific examples of how one person can learn many things in a short time while another cannot.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi argues that if the Mu'tazilite claim were true, one could also claim that people are equal in beauty, height, strength, etc., which is obviously false.
This shows the original claim leads to impossible conclusions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that such a doctrine is feigned ignorance and that a rational person should seek refuge from it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أفضى بهم تماديهم في الضلال إلى أن زعموا أن الله تعالى لم يفضل أحد على أحد بإعطاء زيادة العقل وعقول الناس كلها في الخلقة مستوية، مع ما يرى ويعاين من وفور عقول بعض الناس وجودة قرائحهم وقوة أذهانهم، وقلة عقول البعض وبلادة أفهامهم وكلال خواطرهم، بحيث يحرز واحد من أنواع العلوم وفنون الآداب وودائع العقول وخزائن الأفهام في مدة لا يقدر من وقع منه في الطرف الأقصى من البلادة والغباوة على إحراز جزء من أضعاف ألف جزء وفي تلك المدة، ولو لم يكن هذا تجاهلاً فلا تجاهل في العالم، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لآخر أن يقول: إن الله تعالى سوى بين الناس في الحسن والدمامة والطول وقصر القامة وقوة البطش ورباطة الجأش والضعف والجبن وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى كثرة، وكل ذلك تجاهل، ومذهب يفضي بصاحبه إلى ارتكاب مثل هذه المحالات ويبدو عواره للبداهة كل هذا الظهور، فكان حقيقا على العاقل أن يستعيذ بالله تعالى منه ويسأل العصمة عن الوقوع فيه وفي أمثاله. ثم لما كان من مذهبهم أن ليس لله تعالى أن يختص برحمته ونعمته وتوفيقه وإصلاحه من يشاء بل الواجب عليه أن يسوي بين كل من عباده في ذلك إلا ما استحقه أحد بطاعته، فكذلك ليس له أن يخص واحدًا منهم بما لم يفعل بغيره مثله إلا إذا كان ذلك أصلح له في الدين أو استحقه بمعصيته فأما لا على ذلك الوجه فهو محال.
Then their persistence in error led them to claim that God Almighty did not favor anyone over anyone by giving an increase of intellect, and that the intellects of all people in creation are equal, despite what is seen and observed of the abundance of intellect of some people, the excellence of their minds, the strength of their understandings, and the scarcity of intellect of others, the dullness of their comprehensions, and the sluggishness of their thoughts, such that one person acquires types of sciences, arts of literature, deposits of intellect, and treasuries of understanding in a period in which one who is at the extreme limit of dullness and stupidity cannot acquire even a part of a thousandth part in that same period. And if this were not feigned ignorance, there is no feigned ignorance in the world. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible for another to say: God Almighty made people equal in beauty and ugliness, tallness and shortness of stature, strength of assault and firmness of resolve, weakness and cowardice, and other things too numerous to count. All of that is feigned ignorance, and a doctrine that leads its adherent to commit such impossibilities, and its flaw appears to common sense with this complete clarity. So it is incumbent upon a rational person to seek refuge with God from it and to ask for protection from falling into it and its likes. Then, since it is part of their doctrine that God does not have the right to single out with His mercy, grace, success, and reform whom He wills, but rather it is obligatory upon Him to make equal all of His servants in that, except what one deserves by his obedience, so likewise He does not have the right to single out one of them with something He did not do for another similar to it, unless that is more beneficial for him in religion or he deserves it by his disobedience. But if it is not in that way, it is impossible.
Source pages p. 1024

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazilite doctrine regarding the equality of human intellects and God's obligation to treat all equally. The author begins by noting that the Mu'tazilites' persistence in error led them to claim that God does not favor anyone over another by giving an increase of intellect, and that all people are equal in creation with respect to intellect. He then points out the obvious falsity of this claim by appealing to observable reality: we see that some people have abundant intellect, sharp minds, and strong understandings, while others are dull and slow-witted. This disparity is so great that a gifted person can master various sciences and arts in a period in which a dull person cannot acquire even a fraction of that. If this is not considered feigned ignorance, then nothing is. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if one accepts the Mu'tazilite claim about equal intellect, one would have to accept equivalent claims about physical traits like beauty, height, strength, etc., which are clearly not equal. All such claims are feigned ignorance and lead to impossibilities. Therefore, a rational person should seek refuge from such a doctrine. Next, the author draws a parallel to another Mu'tazilite principle: that God must treat all servants equally in mercy and grace unless they merit something by their obedience. He argues that, by the same logic, God would not be allowed to single out anyone with a special gift unless it is for the person's religious benefit or due to disobedience. Otherwise, such special treatment is impossible. This refutation demonstrates the logical consequences of Mu'tazilite principles, showing they lead to absurdities.

Key Terms
العقل
الفضل
التجاهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the Mu'tazilite claim of equal intellect is about social justice or equality of opportunity.
Correction: It is a theological claim about God's creative act, not a social program, and Nasafi refutes it by pointing to observable reality.

Checkpoint
What observable phenomenon does Nasafi use as evidence against the Mu'tazilite claim of equal intellect?
A) Differences in physical strength
B) Differences in beauty and height
C) Differences in intellectual capacity and speed of learning
D) Differences in wealth and status

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's refutation relies on observable reality: people clearly have different intellectual capacities, so the claim of equal intellect is false and leads to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever credited your own intelligence or learning to your personal effort, forgetting that it is a gift from Allah that He withholds from others? What specific moment in your life reveals this?
?
When you encounter someone who struggles to understand what comes easily to you, do you feel superiority or gratitude? How does this lesson's emphasis on God's distribution of intellect challenge your inner reaction?
?
If you honestly examine yourself, do you harbor a secret belief that your intellectual abilities make you more deserving of success or status than others? How would Nasafi's refutation of equal intellect correct that assumption?

Refuting the Doctrine of God's Obligation to Treat All Equally
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite equal treatment using parallel argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that God must treat all servants equally in mercy and grace unless they merit something by obedience.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite principle of equal treatment leads to impossibility.

After refuting the equal intellect claim, Nasafi turns to another Mu'tazilite doctrine: that God cannot single out anyone with His mercy, grace, or favor unless that person deserves it by obedience. Nasafi argues by drawing a parallel to special treatment: if God must treat all equally, then He cannot give any special gift unless it is for the person's religious benefit or due to disobedience. Otherwise, it is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazilite principle
The Mu'tazilites held that God must treat all servants equally in mercy, grace, and guidance, except when someone deserves something by obedience.
2
Follow Nasafi's parallel argument
Nasafi argues that by the same principle, God cannot single out anyone with something He did not give to another, unless it is more beneficial for that person's religion or due to disobedience.
This shows the principle leads to restricting God's freedom.
3
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi declares that any special treatment not on those grounds is impossible according to their own logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أفضى بهم تماديهم في الضلال إلى أن زعموا أن الله تعالى لم يفضل أحد على أحد بإعطاء زيادة العقل وعقول الناس كلها في الخلقة مستوية، مع ما يرى ويعاين من وفور عقول بعض الناس وجودة قرائحهم وقوة أذهانهم، وقلة عقول البعض وبلادة أفهامهم وكلال خواطرهم، بحيث يحرز واحد من أنواع العلوم وفنون الآداب وودائع العقول وخزائن الأفهام في مدة لا يقدر من وقع منه في الطرف الأقصى من البلادة والغباوة على إحراز جزء من أضعاف ألف جزء وفي تلك المدة، ولو لم يكن هذا تجاهلاً فلا تجاهل في العالم، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لآخر أن يقول: إن الله تعالى سوى بين الناس في الحسن والدمامة والطول وقصر القامة وقوة البطش ورباطة الجأش والضعف والجبن وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى كثرة، وكل ذلك تجاهل، ومذهب يفضي بصاحبه إلى ارتكاب مثل هذه المحالات ويبدو عواره للبداهة كل هذا الظهور، فكان حقيقا على العاقل أن يستعيذ بالله تعالى منه ويسأل العصمة عن الوقوع فيه وفي أمثاله. ثم لما كان من مذهبهم أن ليس لله تعالى أن يختص برحمته ونعمته وتوفيقه وإصلاحه من يشاء بل الواجب عليه أن يسوي بين كل من عباده في ذلك إلا ما استحقه أحد بطاعته، فكذلك ليس له أن يخص واحدًا منهم بما لم يفعل بغيره مثله إلا إذا كان ذلك أصلح له في الدين أو استحقه بمعصيته فأما لا على ذلك الوجه فهو محال.
Then their persistence in error led them to claim that God Almighty did not favor anyone over anyone by giving an increase of intellect, and that the intellects of all people in creation are equal, despite what is seen and observed of the abundance of intellect of some people, the excellence of their minds, the strength of their understandings, and the scarcity of intellect of others, the dullness of their comprehensions, and the sluggishness of their thoughts, such that one person acquires types of sciences, arts of literature, deposits of intellect, and treasuries of understanding in a period in which one who is at the extreme limit of dullness and stupidity cannot acquire even a part of a thousandth part in that same period. And if this were not feigned ignorance, there is no feigned ignorance in the world. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible for another to say: God Almighty made people equal in beauty and ugliness, tallness and shortness of stature, strength of assault and firmness of resolve, weakness and cowardice, and other things too numerous to count. All of that is feigned ignorance, and a doctrine that leads its adherent to commit such impossibilities, and its flaw appears to common sense with this complete clarity. So it is incumbent upon a rational person to seek refuge with God from it and to ask for protection from falling into it and its likes. Then, since it is part of their doctrine that God does not have the right to single out with His mercy, grace, success, and reform whom He wills, but rather it is obligatory upon Him to make equal all of His servants in that, except what one deserves by his obedience, so likewise He does not have the right to single out one of them with something He did not do for another similar to it, unless that is more beneficial for him in religion or he deserves it by his disobedience. But if it is not in that way, it is impossible.
Source pages p. 1024

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazilite doctrine regarding the equality of human intellects and God's obligation to treat all equally. The author begins by noting that the Mu'tazilites' persistence in error led them to claim that God does not favor anyone over another by giving an increase of intellect, and that all people are equal in creation with respect to intellect. He then points out the obvious falsity of this claim by appealing to observable reality: we see that some people have abundant intellect, sharp minds, and strong understandings, while others are dull and slow-witted. This disparity is so great that a gifted person can master various sciences and arts in a period in which a dull person cannot acquire even a fraction of that. If this is not considered feigned ignorance, then nothing is. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if one accepts the Mu'tazilite claim about equal intellect, one would have to accept equivalent claims about physical traits like beauty, height, strength, etc., which are clearly not equal. All such claims are feigned ignorance and lead to impossibilities. Therefore, a rational person should seek refuge from such a doctrine. Next, the author draws a parallel to another Mu'tazilite principle: that God must treat all servants equally in mercy and grace unless they merit something by their obedience. He argues that, by the same logic, God would not be allowed to single out anyone with a special gift unless it is for the person's religious benefit or due to disobedience. Otherwise, such special treatment is impossible. This refutation demonstrates the logical consequences of Mu'tazilite principles, showing they lead to absurdities.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الفضل
المحال

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, under the Mu'tazilite principle, when is it permissible for God to single out a servant with a special gift?
A) Only if the servant deserves it by obedience
B) Only if it is more beneficial for the servant's religion or deserved by disobedience
C) Only if it is for worldly benefit
D) Only if it is for the sake of all creation

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazilite principle of equal treatment logically restricts God's actions to only those that are religiously beneficial or punitive, which is an impossible constraint on divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's mercy is something you can earn by your obedience, or do you accept that He can grant it to whomever He wills without any cause from you? Think of a time you felt you 'deserved' something from God. What does this lesson say about that feeling? Will you change your attitude?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending God's justice, but they ended up limiting His power. Where in your own life have you imposed conditions on God's mercy, expecting Him to treat you fairly based on your actions? Are you ready to surrender that demand and embrace His absolute freedom?
?
Nasafi shows that the principle of equal treatment leads to impossibility. Are you living with the assumption that God owes you something? What would it mean to truly believe that God owes nothing to anyone, and all His blessings are purely from His grace? How would that change your daily gratitude and supplication?

Children's Suffering and Mu'tazilite Responses
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite objection and two responses with refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite attempts to justify children's suffering and the refutation of their arguments based on their own principles.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite justice principle collapses when faced with innocent suffering.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a critical challenge to Mu'tazilite theology: if God is just and does what is most beneficial, why do innocent children suffer? The Mu'tazilites offer two explanations, but each leads to contradictions within their own system. Let's examine their reasoning and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the initial objection: children suffer without having committed any sin or having religious obligation. This seems to contradict the Mu'tazilite principle that God always does what is most beneficial.
Notice that the objection assumes the suffering is not beneficial for the child.
2
Identify the first response
The first Mu'tazilite group claims children suffer to teach adults a lesson. Read how Nasafi refutes this by pointing out the harm to the child and the contradiction with Mu'tazilite own conditions.
The refutation highlights two internal contradictions for the Mu'tazilites.
3
Identify the second response
The majority of Mu'tazilites claim the suffering is compensated with eternal reward in the Hereafter. Read the beginning of the refutation, which notes that if God did not compensate, He would be unjust.
The passage cuts off here, so we only have the start of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these responses attempt to maintain God's justice but fail according to Mu'tazilite own criteria. Think about why this is a significant challenge for their theology.
This connects to broader debates about divine justice and the problem of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[تألم الأطفال لماذا]؟ فقيل لهم : إن الأطفال يتألمون ويتوجعون وتصيبهم الأمراض والأوجاع، ولم يستوجبوا شيئًا من ذلك بصنيعهم لما لا تصور للجريمة منهم وليس ذلك أصلح لهم في الدين إذ لا تكليف عليهم فتفرقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا. فزعمت طائفة منهم أنهم يؤلمون ليتعظ بذلك الآباء والأمهات ومن عاين ذلك من البالغين. قيل لهم : إن كان ذلك للبالغين أصلح فلا شك أن هذا الطفل يتضرر به في الدنيا، ولا ينتفع به في الآخرة لما لا يصير ذلك داعيًا له إلى فعل طاعة أو الامتناع عن معصية لانعدام عقله للحال وعدم تصور تذكره لذلك عند البلوغ، أو لأنه ربما يموت قبل جري التكليف عليه وإثبات الأصلح لغير الصغير مما [؟] مفسدة للصغير تناقض منكم لوجهين: أحدهما : أن ما فعل بالصغير ليس بأصلح له، فإذا فعل بشخص ما ليس بأصلح له فيكون هذا في حقه ظلما وجورًا عندكم. والآخر أن عندكم من شرط الأصلح أن لا يصير بإعطاء الأصلح خارجًا عن الحكمة، وفي هذا خروجه عن الحكمة. وزعم جمهورهم أن هذا أصلح لهذا الصغير، لأنه يعوضه بذلك ثوابًا مخلدًا في الآخرة ولا يجوز أن يعوضه ولو لم يعوضه لكان ظالما، والإيلام لمنفعة
[Why do children suffer?] So it was said to them: Children suffer, experience pain, and are afflicted with illnesses and aches, and they have not deserved any of that by their own action because no conception of sin exists from them, and that is not more beneficial for them in religion since there is no obligation upon them. Then the Mu'tazilites split in objection to this. One group claimed that they are made to suffer so that parents and mothers and those adults who witness it may take heed. It was said to them: If that is more beneficial for the adults, there is no doubt that this child is harmed by it in this world, and does not benefit from it in the Hereafter, because it does not become an incentive for him to perform an act of obedience or refrain from disobedience, due to the lack of his intellect at present and the lack of conception of his remembering that at puberty, or because he may die before the imposition of obligation upon him. And establishing what is more beneficial for someone other than the young child while it is a [؟] harm for the child is contradictory from you in two ways: One of them: that what was done to the young child is not more beneficial for him, so if something is done to a person that is not more beneficial for him, then that in his case is injustice and oppression according to you. The other: that according to you, one of the conditions of what is more beneficial is that giving what is more beneficial should not result in a departure from wisdom, and in this there is a departure from wisdom. And the majority of them claimed that this is more beneficial for this young child, because he will be compensated for it with an eternal reward in the Hereafter, and it is not permissible that he be compensated, and if He did not compensate him He would be unjust, and inflicting pain for a benefit
Source pages p. 1025

Commentary
This passage presents the theological problem of children's suffering from a Mu'tazilite perspective and the responses of various Mu'tazilite groups. The author first states the objection: children suffer pain and illness without having committed any sin or having any religious obligation, so it seems that the suffering is not for their benefit. The Mu'tazilites then split into two groups in their response. The first group claims that children are made to suffer so that adults, especially parents, may learn a lesson. The author refutes this by arguing that if the suffering is beneficial for the adults, it is still harmful for the child in this world and provides no benefit in the Hereafter, since the child lacks intellect now and may not remember the suffering at puberty, or may die before reaching obligation. Moreover, the author points out a twofold contradiction in this position: (1) what is done to the child is not beneficial for the child, and according to Mu'tazilite principles, doing something not beneficial to someone is unjust; (2) one of the Mu'tazilite conditions for an act to be 'most beneficial' is that it does not lead to a departure from wisdom, but this act does lead to such a departure. The second group, the majority, claims that the suffering is beneficial for the child because he will be compensated with an eternal reward in the Hereafter. The author begins to refute this by noting that if God does not compensate, He would be unjust, but the text is cut off at the page end. This passage shows the Mu'tazilites struggling to reconcile their principle of divine justice with the observed reality of innocent suffering.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazilites deny that children suffer or that they believe suffering is always beneficial for the one suffering.
Correction: The passage shows they accept children suffer and attempt to justify it as beneficial either for adults (first response) or for the child via compensation (second response).

Checkpoint
What is the first Mu'tazilite response to children's suffering mentioned in the passage?
A) Children are compensated in the Hereafter
B) Children suffer because of their own sins
C) Children suffer to teach adults a lesson
D) Children do not actually feel pain

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite attempts to explain innocent suffering fail because they violate their own principles of justice and benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a parent watching your child suffer, would you honestly be able to say, 'This is the most beneficial thing for my child,' as the Mu'tazilites claim? Or would your heart cry out against it? What does that tension reveal about the limits of human theology?
?
The Mu'tazilites built an entire system to defend God's justice, yet it crumbles when faced with innocent suffering. Do you have any beliefs about God that you cling to without questioning, even when life seems to contradict them? Are you willing to bring those beliefs into the light of honest examination?
?
The passage shows that even sincere theological reasoning can lead to contradictions when it tries to explain suffering. How do you personally respond to the reality of unexplainable suffering in your own life or in the world? Is your trust in God conditional on understanding His reasons, or does it survive beyond explanation?

The Mu'tazili Claim and the Problem of Children Dying in Unbelief
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazili claim and gives the first refutation regarding children who die in unbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili analogy of a father treating his child fails to justify God inflicting pain on children who die in unbelief.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of eternal outcomes breaks the Mu'tazili analogy.

This lesson introduces the Mu'tazili claim that God inflicts pain on children in this world for future compensation, analogizing it to a father who causes temporary discomfort for a greater benefit. Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by pointing out a fatal flaw: many children suffer and die in unbelief, and God knows this beforehand. Thus, causing them pain without compensation would be injustice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim and Nasafi's first argument
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Notice how the claim is stated and then immediately refuted.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the analogy (father cupping/giving medicine), the fact about children dying in unbelief, and God's foreknowledge. See how these connect.
3
Reflect on why this refutes the Mu'tazila
Consider why the existence of children who die in unbelief undermines the Mu'tazili claim. Ask yourself: if God knows a child will not be compensated, is it just to cause pain?
This ties to the concept of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحصل في العاقبة به حكمة، كالأب يحجم صبيه ويسقيه الأدوية الكريهة ويحسن ذلك لأن فيه من منفعة البرء في العاقبة. وهذا الكلام باطل من وجوه: أحدها: أن كثيرًا من الأطفال الذين تألموا في صغرهم ماتوا على الكفر ولا ينالون العوض في الآخرة، وكان الله تعالى عالمًا بعاقبة أمرهم عند إيلامهم فيكون الباري عز وجل على هذا ظالمًا على هذا الطفل حيث آلمه علمه أنه لا يعوضه، ولأن ما كان ظلمًا بغير عوض يعتقد ظلمًا وجورًا عند الوجود إلى أن يرضى من له الحق بالعوض، فينعقد فعل الله تعالى للحال ظلمًا ويصير هو ظالمًا إلى أن يزول أثر الظلم بإيصال العوض، وكذا ما يكون ظلمًا بغير عوض لا يزول عنه معنى الظلم، وإن كان الفاعل قصد التعويض ما لم يرض بذلك من له الحق ولا رضا من الصغير وقت الإيلام بالعوض، وما ضرب من المثال بفصل الحجامة وسقي الأدوية فهو الذي يدل على بطلان مذهبهم وفساد قولهم، فإن الأب إنما لم يخرج بذلك عن الحكمة وأطلق له ذلك الفعل إذا كان لا يقدر على تحصيل البرء والصحة بدون سابقة الإيلام بالحجامة والإضرار بسقي الأدوية الكريهة، ولو فعل القدرة على ذلك يعد ظالمًا سفيها جائراً، والله تعالى قادر على إيصال ذلك الذي يوصل إليه بطريق العوض بدون سابقة الإيلام فينبغي أن يكون الإيلام سفها اعتبارًا بالشاهد الذي هو مقوله.
It results in the end as wisdom, like the father who cups his son and gives him unpleasant medicine and does that well because therein lies the benefit of healing in the end. And this statement is false in several respects: First: Many children who suffered pain in their childhood died in unbelief and do not receive compensation in the afterlife, and God Almighty knew the outcome of their affair when He caused them pain. Thus, the Creator, Exalted and Glorified, would be unjust to this child because He caused him pain while knowing that He would not compensate him. And because whatever is injustice without compensation is considered injustice and oppression when it occurs until the one who has the right is satisfied with compensation. So God's action at present becomes injustice, and He becomes unjust until the effect of injustice is removed by delivering compensation. Similarly, what is injustice without compensation does not remove from it the meaning of injustice, even if the doer intended compensation, as long as the one who has the right has not consented, and there is no consent from the child at the time of the pain for compensation. And the example given of cupping and giving medicine indicates the invalidity of their doctrine and the corruption of their statement. For the father only does not depart from wisdom and is permitted that action if he is unable to achieve recovery and health without prior pain from cupping and harm from giving unpleasant medicine. But if he were able to do that, he would be considered unjust, foolish, and oppressive. And God Almighty is able to deliver that which is obtained by way of compensation without prior pain, so the pain would be foolishness by analogy with the witnessed realm, which is his analogy.
Source pages p. 1026

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazili claim that God inflicts pain on children in this world as a means to grant them compensation in the afterlife, analogous to a father causing temporary discomfort for a greater benefit. The author, Imam Nasafi, declares this claim false and presents several arguments. First, he points out that many children who suffer die in unbelief and receive no compensation in the afterlife; God knows this outcome beforehand, so causing them pain without compensation would be injustice. Second, even if compensation is intended, the act is still unjust until compensation is actually delivered and the child's right is satisfied; but the child cannot consent at the time of pain. Third, the analogy of the father fails because the father only inflicts pain when he has no other means to achieve healing; but God is all-powerful and can grant the compensation without any prior pain. Therefore, if God inflicts pain unnecessarily, it would be foolishness, contrary to wisdom.

Key Terms
إيلام الأطفال
عوض
ظلم
حكمة
التمثيل بالشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili analogy is valid because temporary suffering always leads to greater compensation.
Correction: The analogy fails because many children die in unbelief and receive no compensation, and God knows this beforehand, making the suffering unjust.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's first argument, why would God be unjust if He inflicts pain on a child who dies in unbelief?
A) Because the child is too young to understand the purpose.
B) Because God knows the child will not be compensated in the afterlife.
C) Because the child did not commit any sin.
D) Because the father analogy is not applicable.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili analogy fails because many children die in unbelief and God knows they will receive no compensation, making the pain unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a group of non-Muslim children suffering in the news, does your heart ache? Does Nasafi's argument that God knows their end and may not compensate them challenge you to trust His justice even when you cannot see it? Will you make a sincere du'a that Allah's wisdom be made manifest to you?
?
Have you ever felt that God's justice was unfair because you suffered without seeing a clear benefit? According to this lesson, God's knowledge of all outcomes means His actions are just whether or not we see compensation. Can you surrender your need to understand and instead praise His perfect justice?
?
If you had a child who died without believing, would you still affirm that God's actions are just? This lesson forces you to confront that possibility. Are you willing to submit your emotions and doubts to Allah's infinite knowledge? What step will you take today to strengthen your trust in His decree?

The Injustice of Pain Without Compensation and the Failure of the Analogy
Nasafi presents the second and third refutations: injustice without consent and the failure of the father analogy due to God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's arguments that pain without compensation is inherently unjust and that God's omnipotence undercuts the Mu'tazili analogy.

Core Idea
Even intended compensation does not erase injustice, and God can grant benefits without pain.

In this lesson, we continue Nasafi's refutation of the Mu'tazili claim. He argues that an act of inflicting pain without compensation is considered unjust until the compensation is actually delivered and the victim consents—but a child cannot consent. Furthermore, the analogy of the father fails because the father acts out of necessity; God, being all-powerful, has no need to use pain as a means. Thus, the Mu'tazili position amounts to attributing foolishness to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second and third arguments carefully
Read the Arabic passage for this lesson. Notice how Nasafi continues with 'And because...' and then 'And the example given...'
2
Analyze the second argument: injustice without consent
Nasafi argues that even if compensation is intended, the act of inflicting pain remains unjust until the compensation is delivered and the victim consents. A child cannot consent at the time of pain.
Consider the legal principle that an act is judged at its occurrence.
3
Analyze the third argument: the father analogy fails
The father's infliction of pain is justified only because he cannot achieve the healing otherwise. God, being all-powerful, can grant the compensation without any prior pain. So inflicting pain would be foolish.
This shows the disanalogy due to God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحصل في العاقبة به حكمة، كالأب يحجم صبيه ويسقيه الأدوية الكريهة ويحسن ذلك لأن فيه من منفعة البرء في العاقبة. وهذا الكلام باطل من وجوه: أحدها: أن كثيرًا من الأطفال الذين تألموا في صغرهم ماتوا على الكفر ولا ينالون العوض في الآخرة، وكان الله تعالى عالمًا بعاقبة أمرهم عند إيلامهم فيكون الباري عز وجل على هذا ظالمًا على هذا الطفل حيث آلمه علمه أنه لا يعوضه، ولأن ما كان ظلمًا بغير عوض يعتقد ظلمًا وجورًا عند الوجود إلى أن يرضى من له الحق بالعوض، فينعقد فعل الله تعالى للحال ظلمًا ويصير هو ظالمًا إلى أن يزول أثر الظلم بإيصال العوض، وكذا ما يكون ظلمًا بغير عوض لا يزول عنه معنى الظلم، وإن كان الفاعل قصد التعويض ما لم يرض بذلك من له الحق ولا رضا من الصغير وقت الإيلام بالعوض، وما ضرب من المثال بفصل الحجامة وسقي الأدوية فهو الذي يدل على بطلان مذهبهم وفساد قولهم، فإن الأب إنما لم يخرج بذلك عن الحكمة وأطلق له ذلك الفعل إذا كان لا يقدر على تحصيل البرء والصحة بدون سابقة الإيلام بالحجامة والإضرار بسقي الأدوية الكريهة، ولو فعل القدرة على ذلك يعد ظالمًا سفيها جائراً، والله تعالى قادر على إيصال ذلك الذي يوصل إليه بطريق العوض بدون سابقة الإيلام فينبغي أن يكون الإيلام سفها اعتبارًا بالشاهد الذي هو مقوله.
It results in the end as wisdom, like the father who cups his son and gives him unpleasant medicine and does that well because therein lies the benefit of healing in the end. And this statement is false in several respects: First: Many children who suffered pain in their childhood died in unbelief and do not receive compensation in the afterlife, and God Almighty knew the outcome of their affair when He caused them pain. Thus, the Creator, Exalted and Glorified, would be unjust to this child because He caused him pain while knowing that He would not compensate him. And because whatever is injustice without compensation is considered injustice and oppression when it occurs until the one who has the right is satisfied with compensation. So God's action at present becomes injustice, and He becomes unjust until the effect of injustice is removed by delivering compensation. Similarly, what is injustice without compensation does not remove from it the meaning of injustice, even if the doer intended compensation, as long as the one who has the right has not consented, and there is no consent from the child at the time of the pain for compensation. And the example given of cupping and giving medicine indicates the invalidity of their doctrine and the corruption of their statement. For the father only does not depart from wisdom and is permitted that action if he is unable to achieve recovery and health without prior pain from cupping and harm from giving unpleasant medicine. But if he were able to do that, he would be considered unjust, foolish, and oppressive. And God Almighty is able to deliver that which is obtained by way of compensation without prior pain, so the pain would be foolishness by analogy with the witnessed realm, which is his analogy.
Source pages p. 1026

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazili claim that God inflicts pain on children in this world as a means to grant them compensation in the afterlife, analogous to a father causing temporary discomfort for a greater benefit. The author, Imam Nasafi, declares this claim false and presents several arguments. First, he points out that many children who suffer die in unbelief and receive no compensation in the afterlife; God knows this outcome beforehand, so causing them pain without compensation would be injustice. Second, even if compensation is intended, the act is still unjust until compensation is actually delivered and the child's right is satisfied; but the child cannot consent at the time of pain. Third, the analogy of the father fails because the father only inflicts pain when he has no other means to achieve healing; but God is all-powerful and can grant the compensation without any prior pain. Therefore, if God inflicts pain unnecessarily, it would be foolishness, contrary to wisdom.

حكمة
التمثيل بالشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: As long as God intends to compensate the child in the afterlife, the act of inflicting pain is not unjust.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the act is unjust at the moment it occurs until compensation is delivered and consent is given; the child's inability to consent means the injustice persists.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's third argument, why does the father analogy actually disprove the Mu'tazili claim?
A) Because the father is always just regardless of his power.
B) Because the father inflicts pain only out of necessity, but God has no such necessity.
C) Because the child must consent to the father's actions.
D) Because the father's act is not analogous to God's act.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili analogy is invalid because God can grant benefits without pain; the father's action is only justified by his inability, but God is all-powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial that seems unjust, do you immediately trust that God will compensate you, or does the present pain make you doubt His justice? How does Nasafi's argument about the timing of injustice challenge your response to suffering?
?
Consider a hardship you've faced that felt unnecessary. Do you truly believe God could have granted you the benefit without the pain? And if you struggle to believe that, how does that affect your trust in His wisdom and mercy?
?
If you had the power to remove someone's suffering without any cost, would you choose to let them suffer first to teach them a lesson? How does this reflection expose your own assumptions about God's dealings with you?

Refutation of al-Kaʿbi on Obligation and Blessings
Nasafi refutes al-Kaʿbi's claim that obligation diminishes blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's argument that obligation diminishes blessings and Nasafi's initial refutation that God's obligation enhances joy rather than diminishing it.

Core Idea
God's obligation (minna) magnifies blessings, not diminishes them.

In this passage, Nasafi engages with an opponent's claim about the nature of divine blessings. Al-Ka'bi argues that a blessing given as a reward is better than a gratuitous gift because the latter carries an obligation that spoils it. Nasafi responds by pointing out that this reasoning fails to account for God's majesty: all blessings come from God, and His obligation upon us is the greatest source of joy. The Qur'anic verse about guidance as a favor supports this. The refutation is incomplete but the key points are clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and locate the two positions
Identify al-Ka'bi's objection (starting from فاعترض الكعبي) and Nasafi's response (starting from قيل). Notice the structure: objection then refutation.
The refutation cuts off mid-sentence, so we focus on what is present.
2
Understand al-Ka'bi's argument
Al-Ka'bi claims that a gratuitous gift carries obligation (minna) which diminishes the blessing, whereas a reward (compensation) has no obligation, making it more enjoyable. Grasp this cause-effect logic.
3
Analyze Nasafi's counterarguments
Nasafi argues that all blessings are from God, His obligation is the greatest, and it actually increases joy. He cites the Quranic verse (49:17) to show that obligation from God is a blessing, not a spoiler. Then he introduces an analysis of why obligation spoils in human affairs: equality of essence but disparity in status, which does not apply to God.
Note that Nasafi uses an analogy from the witnessed (shahid) realm to explain the cause, but concludes it does not apply to God.
4
Reflect on the incomplete refutation
The passage ends with 'ثم إن الفائز بالنعم' (Then the one who attains the blessings...). The full refutation likely continues. Consider what additional points Nasafi might have made.
This lesson covers only the opening part of the refutation.

فاعترض الكعبي على هذا، وزعم أن إعطاء ذلك بطريق الثواب أنفع للصبي من إعطائه ابتداء بالتفضل لما في الإعطاء ابتداء من لحوق المنة وفي الوصول بطريق العوض من ارتفاعها، لما لا منة فيما ينال بطريق الأعواض، والمنة تنقص النعمة فيكون ما نيل بطريق العوض أهنأ وألذ. قيل: هذا كلام من عرف الله تعالى لا يستجيز من نفسه أن يخطر بباله فضلاً عن التكلم والاحتجاج بمثله عند ملاقاة الخصوم، وذلك لأن النعم كلها من الله تعالى وله علينا المنة العظمى ونتلذذ بالنعم بامتنانه ومن كانت منته عليه أوفر كان هو بذلك أسر وقلبه أفرح وعيشه ألذ، فلو كانت منته تهدم الصنيعة وتنقص النعمة ينبغي أن تكون نعمة الهداية منغصة مكدرة على المؤمنين حيث قال تعالى: (بَلِ الله يمنُّ عَلَيْكُمْ أَنْ هَدَنكُمْ لِلإِيمَنِ) ثم لو عرف في هذا التائه في دينه المتحير في أمره علة تنغص النعم بالمن في الشاهد لما تكلم بهذا الكلام الشنيع مسمعه الفظيع موقعه، وذلك أن علة تنغص النعمة في الشاهد بلحوق المنة استواء المنعم والمنعم عليه في الجوهر وتفاوتهما في الحال والرتبة، ثم فوز أحدهما بالسعة في المال والرفعة والجلال في الشاهد والحال وحرمان الآخر عن كل ذلك، ثم إن الفائز بالنعم
Then al-Kaʿbi objected to this, and claimed that giving that by way of reward is more beneficial for the child than giving it initially as a gratuitous favor, because in giving initially there is the attachment of obligation, while in obtaining by way of compensation there is its absence, since there is no obligation in what is obtained by way of compensations, and obligation diminishes the blessing, so what is obtained by way of compensation is more enjoyable and pleasant. It was said: This is the speech of one who knows God Almighty and does not permit himself to think, let alone speak and argue with such a thing when meeting opponents. That is because all blessings are from God Almighty, and He has the greatest obligation upon us, and we delight in blessings through His bestowal of obligation. And whoever has more of His obligation upon him is thereby more joyful, his heart happier, and his life more pleasant. So if His obligation destroyed the favor and diminished the blessing, then the blessing of guidance would be bitter and troubled for the believers, since He said Exalted: 'But God bestows obligation upon you that He guided you to faith.' Then if this confused one in his religion, perplexed in his affair, knew the cause that spoils blessings by obligation in the witnessed realm, he would not have uttered this ugly speech with its terrible meaning and grave consequence. And that is because the cause of the spoiling of the blessing in the witnessed realm due to the attachment of obligation is the equality of the benefactor and the beneficiary in essence and their difference in state and rank, then the attainment of one of them of wealth, high status, and majesty in the witnessed realm and situation, and the deprivation of the other of all that. Then the one who attains the blessings...
Source pages p. 1027

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Kaʿbi and the author's refutation. Al-Kaʿbi argues that a blessing given as a reward (compensation) is better than a gratuitous gift because the gratuitous gift carries an obligation (minna) from the giver, which diminishes the blessing, whereas compensation carries no such obligation. Therefore, the child would enjoy a reward more than a free gift. The author responds that this argument is foolish because it fails to recognize God's majesty. All blessings come from God, and His obligation upon us is the greatest; it actually increases joy, not diminishes it. The Qur'anic verse (49:17) confirms that God's obligation of guidance is a blessing. Moreover, in the human realm, obligation spoils a favor only when the giver and receiver are equal in essence but differ in status—something incomparable to God. The answer is incomplete in the text, but the author's point is clear: God's obligation is not diminishing because He is infinitely superior, and the human analogy does not apply to Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any obligation (minna) from God diminishes the blessing He gives.
Correction: God's obligation is a source of joy and increases the blessing, as seen in the Quranic verse where God's bestowal of obligation for guidance is a favor. The human analogy of obligation spoiling blessings does not apply to God.

Checkpoint

B) Gratuitous favor carries obligation which diminishes the blessing.
C) Reward is more certain to be received.
D) Gratuitous favor is not from God.

Key Takeaway
God's obligation upon us is a source of joy and magnifies His blessings, contrary to the claim that obligation diminishes them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's emphasis on God's minna as a source of joy challenge your internal reaction? Will you consciously try to view His commands as a privilege rather than a burden starting today?
?
Consider the blessing of guidance to faith. Have you ever taken it for granted or felt it was merely a duty? How does knowing that God Himself calls it an obligation (minna) change your gratitude? What is one specific way you can express thankfulness for this blessing in the next hour?
?
The passage contrasts human social norms where obligation spoils favors with God's transcendence. Are there areas in your relationship with God where you subconsciously treat Him like a human benefactor—expecting Him to 'owe' you something? How can you purify your heart from such assumptions, and what step will you take this week to remember that His favors are magnified by His obligation, not diminished?

Allah's Obligation Enhances Blessings: Refuting al-Ka'bi
Imam Nasafi refutes al-Ka'bi using Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's obligation (manna) does not diminish blessings, but rather increases them, as demonstrated by the Quranic verse and logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Allah's obligation is a source of joy, not spoilage.

Al-Ka'bi claimed that blessings are diminished when accompanied by obligation (manna). Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by asserting that such a claim stems from ignorance of Allah. He argues that all blessings are from Allah, and His obligation upon us is the greatest. Far from spoiling, His obligation increases our delight. He cites the Quranic verse where Allah says He confers obligation upon the believers for guiding them to faith. In this lesson, we examine the initial refutation and its scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its flow. Notice how al-Nasafi introduces al-Ka'bi's claim and then immediately refutes it with a strong opening.
The passage begins with the heading and then reports al-Ka'bi's objection.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's argument
Al-Ka'bi argues that giving compensation by way of reward is better than giving it as a voluntary favor because obligation (manna) diminishes blessings.
He claims that what is obtained through compensation has no obligation, so it is more wholesome.
3
Analyze the refutation
Al-Nasafi rejects this as the speech of one who does not know Allah. He states that all blessings are from Allah and His obligation is the greatest, increasing joy.
He asks: if obligation spoiled blessings, then the blessing of guidance would be spoiled, but Allah says He confers obligation for guidance.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The verse (49:17) shows that Allah's obligation (manna) for guidance is a favor, not a spoiler. This directly contradicts al-Ka'bi's claim.
The verse proves that divine obligation enhances the value of the blessing.

والرفعة والجلال في الشاهد والحال وحرمان الآخر عن كل ذلك، ثم إن الفائز بالنعم المختص بمزية في السعة والميسرة والجاه والمقدرة اتخذ يدًا بمكان صاحبه وأدر عليه سوابغ نعمه، فيرى المنعم عليه نفسه تحت منته ورهن مباره وأياديه ورقبته كالمسترقة له مع أن تبدل الحال مترتب تحت الإمكان الأقرب والتصور الأيسر فتتنغص عليه النعمة بمقابلة ما يرى برقبته مسترقة لمن يساويه في الرتبة أو يقاربه في الدرجة والمنزلة حتى إن Refutation of al-Ka'bi who says: 'Blessings are diminished by obligation.' So al-Ka'bi objected to this [previous assertion] and claimed that giving that [compensation] by way of reward is more beneficial for the boy than giving it initially by way of grace/voluntary favor, because giving initially entails the attachment of obligation, while obtaining by way of compensation involves its removal, since there is no obligation in what is obtained by way of compensations, and obligation diminishes the blessing; thus what is obtained by way of compensation is more wholesome and delightful. And it was said to him: This is the speech of one who does not know Allah Most High—he would not permit himself to let such a thought cross his mind, let alone speak and argue with it when facing opponents. This is because all blessings are from Allah Most High, and He has the supreme obligation over us, and we delight in blessings through His bestowal of obligation; and one upon whom His obligation is more abundant is thereby more joyous, his heart more happy, and his life more delightful. If His obligation destroyed the favor and diminished the blessing, then the blessing of guidance would necessarily be spoiled and troubled for the believers, since He said: 'But Allah confers obligation upon you that He has guided you to faith.' Moreover, if this confused person in his religion, bewildered in his affair, knew the cause that spoils blessings by obligation in the witnessed world, he would not utter this ugly speech with its terrible sound and [bad] position. For the cause of the spoiling of blessing in the witnessed world by the attachment of obligation is the equality of the benefactor and the benefited in essence, and their difference in state and rank; then the one who wins by abundance in wealth, elevation, and majesty in the witnessed world and state, and the deprivation of the other from all that; then the one who is granted blessings, distinguished by a privilege in abundance, ease, status, and capability, [takes it as] a hand over his companion and causes his abundant blessings to flow upon him. So the one benefited sees himself under his obligation, bound to his favors, benefactions, and acts of kindness, as if he is subservient to him, while the change of state is subject to the nearest possibility and easiest conception, so the blessing becomes spoiled for him in comparison to what he sees as subservient to his neck for one who is equal to him in rank or close to him in degree and station. Until if the change of state were among the most distant possibilities and far-fetched imaginations—such as a king among the kings of the earth or a great one among the greats of the kingdom especially [favors] some commoner, one of the trusted people, and makes him worthy of his beloved ones and favors, and takes him as a confidant for his secrets, or the supreme ruler conveys to him from the greatest of the sons of his state and supporters of his cause enormous wealth, and bestows upon him precious robes of honor—the one benefited would count that among his greatest honors and most magnificent achievements; and the more his fortune increases from that, the more he increases in joy in himself and expansion and openness in his heart. And an example: if one of these great and mighty ones bought something from someone and paid him its price, the seller would not obtain any cause for pride in that, nor would he have any status and worth among people; whereas what is obtained from the ruler by way of bestowal of obligation and favor is more delightful, more desirable, and more wholesome in the opinion of all rational people. And this is the case even though the change of state and alteration of condition is subject to possibility, though distant, and falls within the sphere of conception, though difficult in habits and rare. So how can it be imagined that His blessings—even if small in themselves and few in themselves—are spoiled by the attachment of obligation from the Almighty, the Majestic, the All-Compelling, the All-Powerful, to Whom everything, even if its magnitude is great and its importance immense, is within His dominion and subject to His compulsion? He honors whom He honors by His beneficence and favor, and humiliates whom He humiliates by His dishonor and degradation. There is no end to His kingdom and sovereignty, and no change in the abasement of the servant and his acknowledgment [of dependence]; rather, change and decay enter into the sphere [...]
Source pages p. 1027

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's objection. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, begins by stating the heading: 'Refutation of al-Ka'bi who says: Blessings are diminished by obligation.' Then he reports al-Ka'bi's claim: giving compensation as reward is more beneficial for the boy than giving it initially as a voluntary favor, because the latter involves obligation (manna) which diminishes the blessing, whereas compensation does not. Al-Ka'bi concludes that what is obtained through compensation is more wholesome and delightful. 

The refutation starts with a strong retort: 'This is the speech of one who does not know Allah Most High.' The author argues that all blessings are from Allah, and His obligation (manna) upon us is the greatest. Far from diminishing joy, His obligation increases it. He cites the Quranic verse (49:17): 'But Allah confers obligation upon you that He has guided you to faith.' If obligation spoiled blessings, guidance itself would be spoiled. 

Then the author analyzes the cause of spoiling in the witnessed world (al-shahid): it occurs when the benefactor and recipient are equal in essence (al-jawhar) but differ in rank and state. The beneficiary feels subservient because the benefactor is of the same kind but superior in wealth or status. This causes the blessing to be spoiled. However, this does not apply to Allah, who is not equal to His creatures in essence. 

To illustrate, the author gives an example: if a great king specially favors a commoner, the commoner feels immense joy and pride, not spoiling. The blessing is even more delightful than a commercial transaction where the buyer pays a fair price. The more remote the favor seems, the greater the joy. Therefore, how could anyone imagine that Allah's blessings—however small—are spoiled by His obligation, when He is the Almighty, the All-Compelling, to Whom everything is subject? The passage ends by affirming that Allah's kingdom is eternal and the servant's abasement is perpetual, while change and decay belong to creation.

Key Terms
منة (manna/obligation)
نعمة (blessing)
الثواب (reward)
التفضل (grace/voluntary favor)
العوض (compensation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any form of obligation (manna) always diminishes the value of a blessing.
Correction: Human obligation among equals can spoil, but Allah's obligation is a favor that increases joy, as shown by the Quranic verse.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, what does the Quranic verse (49:17) demonstrate?
A) That Allah's obligation spoils the blessing of guidance.
B) That Allah's obligation is a favor that increases joy.
C) That blessings are diminished by obligation.
D) That human obligation and divine obligation are identical.

Key Takeaway
Allah's obligation is a favor that increases joy, not a burden that spoils blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the obligations Allah has placed on you, do they feel like a burden or a delight? What would it take for you to truly experience them as the greatest favors?
?
Have you ever felt that obeying Allah's commands spoiled your enjoyment of life? How does the verse about guidance challenge that feeling, and what step can you take today to change your perspective?
?
If you truly believed that every obligation from Allah is a gift that increases joy, how would your attitude toward prayer, fasting, and other duties change starting tomorrow?

The Cause of Spoiling in the Witnessed World and Its Inapplicability to Allah
Imam Nasafi analyzes the cause of spoiling and shows it is inapplicable to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational cause of why obligation spoils blessings among human beings and why this cause does not apply to Allah, using the example of a king's favor.

Core Idea
Obligation only spoils among equals; with Allah, it magnifies joy.

After the initial Quranic proof, Imam Nasafi provides a deeper analysis. He explains that in the human realm (al-shahid), obligation spoils blessings because the benefactor and beneficiary are equal in essence but differ in rank—the beneficiary feels subservient. However, this dynamic does not apply to Allah, who is incomparably superior. To illustrate, al-Nasafi gives the example of a king's favor, which brings great joy to the recipient, while a commercial transaction does not. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the cause
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'ثم لو عرف' to the end. Focus on how al-Nasafi explains the cause (علة) of spoiling in the witnessed world.
The passage is long; take your time.
2
Identify the cause of spoiling
The cause is equality in essence (الجوهر) and difference in rank (الرتبة) between benefactor and beneficiary. The beneficiary feels subservient, which spoils the blessing.
This only happens when both are human.
3
Analyze the king's example
Al-Nasafi gives an example: if a king specially favors a commoner, the commoner feels great joy and pride, not spoiling. In contrast, a fair commercial transaction brings no such joy.
The more remote the favor, the greater the delight.
4
Apply the conclusion to Allah
Since Allah is not equal to His creatures in essence, the cause of spoiling does not apply. His obligation enhances the blessing because of His majesty.
The passage ends by emphasizing Allah's absolute power and the servant's perpetual dependence.
